###xml 1188 1203 1174 1189 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" rid="fig5" ref-type="fig">Fig. 5, <italic>h</italic> and <italic>i</italic></xref>
###xml 1205 1212 1191 1198 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="table" rid="tbl3">Table 3</xref>
###xml 1265 1266 1251 1252 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">I</italic>
###xml 1266 1268 1252 1254 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">Ca</sub>
###xml 1278 1281 1264 1267 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">d&#8211;f</italic>
###xml 1286 1287 1272 1273 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">h</italic>
###xml 1292 1293 1278 1279 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">i</italic>
###xml 1270 1293 1256 1279 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" rid="fig5" ref-type="fig">Fig. 5, <italic>d&#8211;f</italic> and <italic>h</italic> and <italic>i</italic></xref>
###xml 1306 1307 1292 1293 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="table" rid="tbl1">1</xref>
###xml 1312 1313 1298 1299 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="table" rid="tbl3">3</xref>
###xml 1319 1320 1305 1306 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">I</italic>
###xml 1320 1322 1306 1308 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">Ba</sub>
###xml 1331 1332 1317 1318 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="table" rid="tbl2">2</xref>
###xml 1337 1338 1323 1324 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="table" rid="tbl3">3</xref>
###xml 1351 1352 1337 1338 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">V</sub>
###xml 1355 1357 1341 1343 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">42</sub>
Intramolecular Protein-Protein Interaction Regulates CaV1.3 Channel Gating-We (15) and others (16) showed that in CaV1.4 alpha1 subunits the distal CTM interacts with a more proximal domain containing the EF-hand, pre-IQ-, and IQ-motif. If such a mechanism also holds true for CaV1.3 channels, CTM-containing peptides should also modulate CaV1.342A (containing EF-PreIQ-IQ). Interestingly, co-expression of GFP-C158 neither reversed the CaV1.342A-induced hyperpolarizing shift of the activation range of ICa (CaV1.342A + GFP-C158: V0.5,act = -12.6 +/- 1.5 mV, n = 7; p < 0.001; see Fig. 5a and Table 1) and IBa (Table 2) nor the voltage-dependent inactivation and CDI (Fig. 5, b and c; Table 3). This prompted us to extend the CaV1.3 alpha1 subunit C terminus to residue 1664 thereby including a highly conserved domain downstream of the IQ-motif that is missing in CaV1.342A and also contains the so-called PCRD domain previously identified as binding partner for C-terminal peptides in CaV1.2 channels (Fig. 6) (17). As expected, the resulting CaV1.3DeltaC473 channels showed CaV1.342A-like gating (Tables 1, 2, 3) but, in contrast to CaV1.342A, co-expression of GFP-C116 restored CDI (Fig. 5, h and i; Table 3) and the activation and inactivation parameters for ICa (Fig. 5, d-f and h and i; and Tables 1 and 3) and IBa (Tables 2 and 3) close to CaV1.342 values. These data provide functional evidence for an important role of proximal residues 1626-1664 and the distal 116 amino acids for CTM function.
###end p 63

###xml 952 953 952 953 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">V</sub>
###xml 992 996 992 996 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">RRQQ</sup>
###xml 992 996 992 996 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink"><sup>RRQQ</sup></italic>
###xml 1097 1100 1097 1100 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">116</sub>
###xml 1119 1122 1119 1122 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">116</sub>
###xml 1122 1126 1122 1126 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">DEQQ</italic>
###xml 1122 1126 1122 1126 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink"><italic>DEQQ</italic></sup>
###xml 1182 1183 1182 1183 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">V</sub>
###xml 1303 1307 1303 1307 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">RRQQ</sup>
###xml 1303 1307 1303 1307 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink"><sup>RRQQ</sup></italic>
###xml 1616 1617 1616 1617 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">V</sub>
###xml 1620 1622 1620 1622 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">42</sub>
###xml 1649 1650 1649 1650 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">V</sub>
To provide further evidence for this mechanism, we measured FRET between different YFP-labeled C-terminal fragments and CFP-labeled C158 (CFP-C158) as well as CFP-labeled C116 (CFP-C116) (Fig. 7). The construct corresponding to the C-terminal fragment of CaV1.342A (YFP-EF-PreIQ-IQ) did not show significant FRET with the CFP-C158 probe (legend to Fig. 7). By extending the CaV1.3 YFP-EF-preIQ-IQ bait to residue 1664 yielding YFP-EF-preIQ-IQ-PCRD, a strong and significant FRET signal was measured with CFP-C158 as well CFP-C116 (Fig. 7), which is in excellent agreement with our electrophysiological data. Accordingly, no FRET was observed when CFP-C76 peptide was used as a probe (not shown). For CaV1.2 channels, interaction between a pair of arginine residues in the PCRD and a set of negatively charged residues in the distal C-terminal region (DCRD) was predicted (Fig. 6) (17). As shown in Fig. 7, mutating the two conserved arginines in the CaV1.3 PCRD to glutamine (EF-preIQ-IQ-PCRDRRQQ containing R1640Q and R1641Q) still supported a significant FRET signal when co-expressed with CFP-C116. Using probe CFP-C116DEQQ, where the two negative charges in DCRD conserved in CaV1.3 were neutralized (D2073Q and E2076Q), significant FRET was eliminated for both EF-preIQ-IQ-PCRD and EF-preIQ-IQ-PCRDRRQQ. Therefore, neutralization of the two positive charges of the PCRD arginines is not sufficient to prevent the binding interaction suggesting that other binding determinants exist in the conserved domain between residues 1626 and 1664. Our data indicate an essential role of the two negative charges in the CaV1.342 DCRD region, similar to CaV1.2.
###end p 64

FIGURE 5.CaV1.3DeltaC473 channel properties in absence and presence of distal CTM peptide C116. Data for CaV1.342 (black) and CaV1.342A (gray) were taken from Figs. 2 and 4. a, representative normalized I-V curves for ICa. Activation parameters (in mV): CaV1.342A: V0.5,act, -14.3, and kact, -6.8; CaV1.342A + GFP-C158: V0.5,act, -13.5 and kact, -7.2; Cells: 196l_32 and 217c_3, respectively. b, representative current traces of ICa evoked a 10-s depolarization to Vmax. Cells: CaV1.342A + GFP-C158, 227k_19; CaV1.342, 316h_51; CaV1.342A, 187g_46. c, percent ICa inactivation during test pulses to Vmax:*, p < 0.05; ***, p < 0.001 compared with CaV1.342; ++, p < 0.01; +++, p < 0.001, comparison of CaV1.342A + GFP-C158 with CaV1.342A alone. d, representative normalized I-V curves for ICa. Activation parameters (in mV): CaV1.3DeltaC473: V0.5,act, -14.4, and kact, -7.1; CaV1.3DeltaC473 +GFP-C116: V0.5,act, -2.4, and kact, -9.4; Cells: 177i_1 and 167j_24, respectively. e, same conditions as in b. Cells: CaV1.3DeltaC473, 247h_25; CaV1.3DeltaC473 +GFP-C116, 167j_14. f, percent ICa inactivation during test pulses to Vmax:*, p < 0.05; **, p < 0.01; ***, p < 0.001 compared with CaV1.342; +, p < 0.05; ++, p < 0.01; +++, p < 0.001, comparison of CaV1.3DeltaC473 + GFP-C116 with CaV1.3DeltaC473 alone. g-i, representative ICa traces for CaV1.342A + GFP-C158 (g), CaV1.3DeltaC473 (h), and CaV1.3DeltaC473 + GFP-C116 (i) correspond to I-V curves in a and d. For comparison to CaV1.342 and CaV1.342A see Fig. 2. For statistics: in a and d, Student's t test; c and f, one-way ANOVA followed by Bonferroni post-test. Number of experiments is given in parentheses. Error bars reflect S.E.

###xml 1480 1481 1444 1445 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">V</sub>

###xml 1492 1498 1456 1462 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" rid="fig2" ref-type="fig">Fig. 2</xref>
###xml 1519 1520 1483 1484 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">a</italic>

###end p 66
###begin p 67
###xml 249 251 249 251 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 383 384 383 384 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">V</sub>
###xml 387 390 387 390 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">42A</sub>
###xml 461 462 461 462 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">V</sub>
###xml 484 485 484 485 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">V</sub>
###xml 549 550 549 550 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">V</sub>
###xml 553 555 553 555 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">42</sub>
###xml 578 579 578 579 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">N</italic>
###xml 579 583 579 583 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">FRET</sub>
###xml 585 586 585 586 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">p</italic>
###xml 595 601 595 601 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">versus</italic>
###xml 608 609 608 609 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">V</sub>
###xml 713 714 711 712 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">n</italic>
###xml 764 765 760 761 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">n</italic>
###xml 787 788 783 784 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">N</italic>
###xml 788 792 784 788 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">FRET</sub>
###xml 816 817 812 813 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">V</sub>
###xml 850 851 844 845 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">n</italic>
###xml 857 858 851 852 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">p</italic>
###xml 878 879 872 873 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">t</italic>
###xml 967 968 961 962 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">V</sub>
###xml 982 984 976 978 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="ref15">15</xref>
###xml 1008 1009 1002 1003 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">V</sub>
To address the question if the moderation of CDI by the CTM involves modulation of CaM interaction with its C-terminal interaction domains, we measured if the presence of the CTM can affect the constitutive binding of CaM at resting intracellular Ca2+ concentrations. We measured FRET between CFP-labeled calmodulin (CFP-CaM) and the fragments YFP-EF-preIQ-IQ (mimicking the short CaV1.342A), YFP-EF-preIQ-IQ-PCRD, and a peptide corresponding to the complete CaV1.3 C terminus (YFP-CaV1.3-C terminus), including the CTM (corresponding to the long CaV1.342). Whereas significant NFRET (p < 0.001 versus YFP-CaV1.3-C terminus; one-way ANOVA, Bonferroni post-test) was measured for YFP-EF-preIQ-IQ (0.185 +/- 0.009, n = 12) and YFP-EF-preIQ-IQ-PCRD (0.235 +/- 0.007, n = 3), no significant NFRET was obtained for YFP-CaV1.3-C terminus (0.039 +/- 0.017, n = 6, p = 0.07. one-sample t test, compared with 0). This is in excellent agreement with our previous findings in CaV1.4 channels (15) indicating that the CaV1.3 CTM interferes with CaM coordination to the C terminus in intact cells. This also provides a plausible mechanism for explaining the moderation of CDI.
###end p 67
###begin p 68
###xml 143 149 143 149 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" rid="fig6" ref-type="fig">Fig. 6</xref>
###xml 227 228 227 228 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">V</sub>
###xml 245 246 245 246 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">V</sub>
###xml 249 254 249 254 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">42+44</sub>
###xml 301 302 301 302 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">V</sub>
###xml 305 307 305 307 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">42</sub>
###xml 309 310 309 310 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">I</italic>
###xml 310 312 310 312 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">Ba</sub>
###xml 314 315 314 315 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">V</italic>
###xml 315 319 315 319 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">0.5,</sub>
###xml 319 322 319 322 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">act</sub>
###xml 343 344 341 342 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">k</italic>
###xml 344 347 342 345 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">act</sub>
###xml 367 368 363 364 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">n</italic>
###xml 375 376 371 372 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">I</italic>
###xml 376 378 372 374 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">Ca</sub>
###xml 380 381 376 377 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">V</italic>
###xml 381 385 377 381 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">0.5,</sub>
###xml 385 388 381 384 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">act</sub>
###xml 409 410 403 404 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">k</italic>
###xml 410 413 404 407 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">act</sub>
###xml 434 435 426 427 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">n</italic>
We also investigated whether the function of the CTM was prevented by alternative splicing of exon 44 in the post-IQ region of the C terminus (Fig. 6). Introduction of 9 amino acid corresponding to exon 44 into a full-length CaV1.3 construct (CaV1.342+44) exhibited activation parameters similar to CaV1.342 (IBa: V0.5,act = -14.8 +/- 1.2 mV, kact = -9.3 +/- 0.3 mV, n = 14; ICa, V0.5,act = -3.7 +/- 1.02 mV, kact = -10.6 +/- 0.3 mV, n = 8) suggesting that CTM function was not prevented by alternative splicing of this exon.
###end p 68
###begin p 69
###xml 2 3 2 3 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">V</sub>
###xml 6 8 6 8 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">42</sub>
###xml 15 16 15 16 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">V</sub>
###xml 19 22 19 22 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">42A</sub>
###xml 0 68 0 68 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">Ca<sub>V</sub>1.3<sub>42</sub> and Ca<sub>V</sub>1.3<sub>42A</sub> Are Both Expressed in Mouse and Human Tissues</italic>
###xml 335 336 335 336 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">a</italic>
###xml 329 336 329 336 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" rid="fig8" ref-type="fig">Fig. 8<italic>a</italic></xref>
###xml 400 401 400 401 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">b</italic>
###xml 394 401 394 401 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" rid="fig8" ref-type="fig">Fig. 8<italic>b</italic></xref>
###xml 561 562 561 562 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">c</italic>
###xml 555 562 555 562 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" rid="fig8" ref-type="fig">Fig. 8<italic>c</italic></xref>
###xml 1005 1006 1003 1004 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">n</italic>
###xml 1139 1140 1135 1136 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">n</italic>
###xml 1188 1189 1182 1183 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">n</italic>
###xml 1243 1244 1235 1236 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">n</italic>
###xml 1287 1288 1279 1280 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">V</sub>
###xml 1349 1350 1341 1342 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">V</sub>
###xml 1481 1482 1473 1474 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">V</sub>
###xml 1485 1488 1477 1480 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">42A</sub>
###xml 1545 1546 1537 1538 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">V</sub>
###xml 45 50 <span type="species:ncbi:10090">Mouse</span>
###xml 55 60 <span type="species:ncbi:9606">Human</span>
###xml 192 197 <span type="species:ncbi:10090">mouse</span>
###xml 202 207 <span type="species:ncbi:9606">human</span>
###xml 285 290 <span type="species:ncbi:10090">mouse</span>
###xml 352 357 <span type="species:ncbi:9606">human</span>
###xml 499 504 <span type="species:ncbi:10090">mouse</span>
CaV1.342 and CaV1.342A Are Both Expressed in Mouse and Human Tissues-We employed PCR to determine whether both long (CTM-containing) and short (CTM-deficient) splice variants are expressed in mouse and human tissue. RT-PCR experiments identified both exon 42 and 42A mRNA in different mouse brain regions and the organ of Corti (Fig. 8a) as well as in human brain, heart, retina, and pancreas (Fig. 8b). Using quantitative real time PCR, we determined the relative abundance of these transcripts in mouse whole-brain as well as several brain sub-regions (Fig. 8c). Because exons 42 and 42A are used in a mutually exclusive manner, we calculated the relative amount of exon 42A as percentage of the sum of exon 42 and 42A containing mRNA molecules in samples from two to three independent RNA preparations. Although exon 42 was the predominant form in whole-brain and all sub-regions investigated, significant expression of exon 42A was also found. In whole-brain preparations, exon 42A was 11.0 +/- 0.6% (n = 8) of the two mRNAs. Variable expression was found in brain sub-regions with the highest expression in cerebellum (17.1 +/- 2.5%, n = 4) and the nucleus accumbens (13.3 +/- 1.5%, n = 3) and the lowest in olfactory bulb (2.1 +/- 0.2%, n = 3). These data suggest that the short CaV1.3 splice variant can contribute significantly to overall CaV1.3 activity. Notably, our experiments do not rule out the possibility of other short isoforms with gating properties similar to CaV1.342A. Therefore the contribution of short forms to overall CaV1.3 expression is likely to be underestimated by our experiments.
###end p 69

###begin title 72
DISCUSSION
###end title 72
###begin p 73
###xml 104 106 104 106 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 140 141 140 141 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">V</sub>
###xml 197 198 197 198 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">V</sub>
###xml 272 273 272 273 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">V</sub>
###xml 276 279 276 279 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">42A</sub>
###xml 315 316 315 316 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">V</sub>
###xml 332 333 332 333 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">V</sub>
###xml 336 339 336 339 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">42A</sub>
###xml 374 375 374 375 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">V</sub>
###xml 378 380 378 380 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">42</sub>
###xml 501 502 501 502 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">V</sub>
###xml 610 611 610 611 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">V</sub>
###xml 645 646 645 646 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="ref6">6</xref>
###xml 766 768 766 768 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="ref30">30</xref>
###xml 770 772 770 772 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="ref31">31</xref>
###xml 802 803 802 803 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="ref7">7</xref>
###xml 805 807 805 807 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="ref32">32</xref>
###xml 837 838 837 838 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">V</italic>
###xml 838 845 838 845 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">0.5,act</sub>
###xml 1158 1159 1158 1159 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">V</sub>
###xml 1162 1165 1162 1165 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">42A</sub>
###xml 1284 1285 1284 1285 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">V</sub>
###xml 1505 1506 1501 1502 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">V</sub>
###xml 1509 1518 1505 1510 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">&#916;C116</sub>
###xml 1522 1523 1514 1515 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">V</sub>
###xml 1526 1535 1518 1523 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">&#916;C158</sub>
###xml 1543 1544 1531 1532 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">V</sub>
###xml 1547 1556 1535 1540 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">&#916;C473</sub>
###xml 1623 1624 1607 1608 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">V</sub>
###xml 394 399 <span type="species:ncbi:10090">mouse</span>
###xml 404 409 <span type="species:ncbi:9606">human</span>
Here we present functional evidence for a C-terminal automodulatory domain that controls voltage- and Ca2+-dependent gating properties of CaV1.3 LTCCs. Such modulation has not been described for CaV1.3 channels before. The absence of the CTM in the short splice variant CaV1.342A provides functional diversity to CaV1.3 channels. CaV1.342A mRNA is expressed together with CaV1.342 in different mouse and human tissues suggesting that this functional diversity is of physiological relevance to adapt CaV1.3 channel activity for specific physiological needs. Activation at negative voltages is one hallmark of CaV1.3 channels (for review see Ref. 6) crucial for adequate neurotransmitter release in IHCs and pacemaker function in the SAN, atrioventricular conduction (30, 31), and neuronal excitability (7, 32). In the absence of the CTM, V0.5,act is shifted about 10 mV to more negative voltages by decreasing the slope factor of the activation curve but without measurable effects on the activation threshold. This effect was not correlated with current densities, was independent from cell passage number, and was reproducibly observed for splice variant CaV1.342A as well as for several truncations mutants. Moreover, it could not be attributed to artificial manipulations of the CaV1.3alpha1 subunits because it was observed in wild-type channel constructs not modified by fusion to GFP or introduction of other tags. In addition, co-expression of peptides containing the CTM with truncation mutants CaV1.3DeltaC116, CaV1.3DeltaC158, and CaV1.3DeltaC473 reversed this shift. Cells may therefore adjust the activity of CaV1.3-mediated signaling by varying the relative abundance of CTM-containing splice variants.
###end p 73

###xml 1234 1235 1230 1231 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">V</italic>
###xml 1235 1238 1231 1234 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">max</sub>

###xml 1251 1253 1247 1249 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">Ba</sub>
###xml 1312 1313 1308 1309 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">V</sub>
###xml 1316 1319 1312 1315 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">42A</sub>
###xml 1330 1331 1326 1327 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="ref5">5</xref>
###xml 1333 1335 1329 1331 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="ref13">13</xref>
###xml 1337 1339 1333 1335 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="ref12">12</xref>
###xml 1461 1462 1457 1458 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">V</sub>
###xml 1528 1532 1524 1528 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">e.g.</italic>
###xml 277 280 <span type="species:ncbi:10116">rat</span>
###xml 599 613 <span type="species:ncbi:8355">Xenopus laevis</span>
###xml 1077 1080 <span type="species:ncbi:10116">rat</span>
###xml 1304 1309 <span type="species:ncbi:9606">human</span>
###xml 1445 1448 <span type="species:ncbi:10116">rat</span>
###xml 1453 1458 <span type="species:ncbi:9606">human</span>
The functional properties of CaV1.342 and CaV1.342A channels have been studied in previous reports. In two studies functional differences were observed between these splice variants, termed CaV1.3a and CaV1.3b therein, respectively. Calin-Jageman et al. (39) found that VDF of rat CaV1.3 channels expressed in HEK-293T cells was significantly more pronounced for CaV1.342A than for CaV1.342 suggesting an autoinhibitory effect of the C-terminal tail on VDF. Comparisons of I-V relationship and ICa were not reported in this study. Zhang et al. (37) expressed hemagglutinin-tagged splice variants in Xenopus laevis oocytes and found that IBa of the CaV1.342A construct was not only larger in amplitude but also its I-V relationship was shifted toward more negative voltages, similar to our findings. ICa was not reported. Although a more detailed comparison of the biophysical properties of CaV1.342 and CaV1.342A was not the purpose of these studies, they support a modulatory role of the C terminus. In contrast, other groups using the corresponding (5, 13) or different (33) rat CaV1.342 and CaV1.342A alpha1 subunit analogues found neither major differences in the activation voltage range nor in CDI (13). Therefore, V0.5,act and Vmax values for IBa as well as the extent of CDI closely resemble our human CaV1.342A channels (5, 13, 12). Even though at present the molecular basis for this difference is unclear, chimeric constructs between rat and human CaV1.3 constructs expressed under identical experimental conditions (e.g. to exclude confounding effects of the heterologous expression system and/or accessory subunit composition) will help to clarify this question.
###end p 78
###begin p 79
###xml 55 56 55 56 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">V</sub>
###xml 155 157 155 157 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="ref15">15</xref>
###xml 159 161 159 161 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="ref17">17</xref>
###xml 216 217 216 217 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">V</sub>
###xml 479 481 475 477 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="ref17">17</xref>
###xml 499 500 495 496 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">V</sub>
###xml 970 973 966 969 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">116</sub>
###xml 491 496 <span type="species:ncbi:9606">human</span>
Our observation of a modulatory C-terminal domain in CaV1.3 adds to recent discoveries of a similar regulatory principle in other LTCC isoforms (see Refs. 15, 17, and references therein). Autoinhibitory control of CaV1.2 channel function was proposed to be based on a binding interaction between a pair of exposed arginine residues and negatively charged residues in alpha-helical motifs in a proximal (PCRD) and a distal (DCRD) conserved region of the C terminus, respectively (17). In our human CaV1.3 channels, 39 residues (1626-1664) comprising the proximal conserved domain containing the PCRD were necessary to confer modulation, as well as FRET to co-expressed CTM-containing peptides. Neutralization of the two conserved positive charges in PCRD did not prevent FRET implying other essential motifs within this 39-residue PCRD domain. Evidence for a role of DCRD comes from the finding that two conserved negative charges are required to support binding of CFP-C116 to the upstream domain.
###end p 79
###begin p 80
###xml 80 81 80 81 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">V</sub>
###xml 91 92 91 92 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">V</sub>
###xml 111 112 111 112 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">V</sub>
###xml 171 172 171 172 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">V</italic>
###xml 172 179 172 179 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">0.5,act</sub>
###xml 354 356 354 356 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="ref17">17</xref>
###xml 384 385 384 385 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">V</sub>
###xml 395 396 395 396 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">V</sub>
###xml 536 538 536 538 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="ref15">15</xref>
###xml 746 747 746 747 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">V</sub>
###xml 752 754 752 754 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="ref15">15</xref>
###xml 784 785 784 785 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">V</sub>
###xml 871 872 871 872 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">V</sub>
The functional consequences of LTCC C-terminal modulation appear different in CaV1.2 and CaV1.3 channels. In CaV1.2, C-terminal truncation does not induce a more negative V0.5,act, but overexpression of the distal C terminus shifts the activation range to more positive voltages and reduces the coupling efficiency of voltage-sensing to channel opening (17). This is in contrast to CaV1.4 and CaV1.3 channels in which removal of the CTM facilitates activation within a more negative voltage range and CTM co-expression restores gating (15 and this paper). Differences between LTCCs also exist with respect to the moderation of CDI. Although in both channels the CTM is able to interfere with CaM association, the CTM completely prevents CDI in CaV1.4 (15) but only moderates CDI in CaV1.3. To our knowledge the effects of the C-terminal autoinhibitory domain on CDI of CaV1.2 channels has not been systematically analyzed so far.
###end p 80
###begin p 81
###xml 42 43 42 43 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">V</sub>
###xml 191 192 191 192 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">V</sub>
###xml 202 203 202 203 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">V</sub>
###xml 217 219 217 219 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="ref18">18</xref>
###xml 221 223 221 223 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="ref34">34</xref>
###xml 232 233 232 233 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">V</sub>
###xml 348 350 348 350 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="ref17">17</xref>
###xml 483 485 479 481 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="ref19">19</xref>
###xml 522 523 518 519 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">V</sub>
###xml 626 628 622 624 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="ref19">19</xref>
###xml 730 731 726 727 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">V</sub>
###xml 791 792 787 788 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">V</sub>
###xml 799 800 795 796 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">V</sub>
###xml 810 811 806 807 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">V</sub>
Our finding of C-terminal modulation of CaV1.3 also raises the important question about potential post-translational proteolytic cleavage in the long C terminus. Such has been reported for CaV1.1 and CaV1.2 channels (18, 34). The CaV1.2 cleavage product not only serves as a potent autoinhibitor when it remains noncovalently bound to the channel (17) but may also dissociate from the alpha1 subunit thereby serving as a transcriptional regulator after translocation to the nucleus (19). So far biochemical evidence for CaV1.3 C-terminal cleavage is lacking, and it does not appear to function as a transcriptional regulator (19). Instead, as demonstrated here, alternative splicing can generate short and functionally distinct CaV1.3 variants. Such splicing has not yet been reported for CaV1.1, CaV1.2, or CaV1.4 channels.
###end p 81
###begin p 82
###xml 42 43 42 43 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">V</sub>
###xml 46 49 46 49 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">42A</sub>
###xml 82 84 82 84 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 101 102 101 102 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">V</sub>
###xml 207 208 207 208 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">e</italic>
###xml 201 208 201 208 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" rid="fig2" ref-type="fig">Fig. 2<italic>e</italic></xref>
###xml 393 395 393 395 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 455 456 455 456 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">V</sub>
###xml 482 486 482 486 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">e.g.</italic>
###xml 540 542 540 542 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="ref36">36</xref>
###xml 592 593 592 593 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">V</sub>
###xml 656 658 656 658 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 764 766 764 766 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 821 823 821 823 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="ref36">36</xref>
###xml 852 853 852 853 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">V</sub>
###xml 856 858 856 858 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">42</sub>
###xml 905 907 905 907 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 1041 1043 1041 1043 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="ref37">37</xref>
Our data predict that the expression of CaV1.342A would allow a cell to promote Ca2+ entry through CaV1.3 channels at sub-threshold voltages as predicted from the negative shift of the window current (Fig. 2e). Stronger activation at more negative voltages may also facilitate the onset of upstate potentials in neurons. However, during maintained depolarization, the faster CDI would limit Ca2+ entry through these channels as relevant in neurons with CaV1.3-dependent pacemaking, e.g. dopamine-containing neurons in the substantia nigra (36). Whereas negative activation of an even small CaV1.3 current could trigger pacemaking, faster CDI would limit Ca2+ entry during ensuing action potentials. This may be important in these neurons that are susceptible to Ca2+ toxicity and neuro-degeneration in Parkinson disease (36). In contrast, the CTM in CaV1.342 channels may be required for longer lasting Ca2+ signals triggered by stronger depolarization inducing cAMP-response element-binding protein phosphorylation and synaptic plasticity (37).
###end p 82
###begin p 83
###xml 150 151 150 151 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">V</sub>
###xml 154 157 154 157 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">42A</sub>
###xml 255 261 255 261 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" rid="fig2" ref-type="fig">Fig. 2</xref>
###xml 308 312 308 312 <italic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">e.g.</italic>
###xml 542 543 542 543 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">V</sub>
###xml 597 598 597 598 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">V</sub>
###xml 763 765 763 765 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="ref38">38</xref>
Our study raises several important questions that need to be addressed in future studies. The fact that the increase in current density observed in CaV1.342A channels seems not to be due to increased expression density (and increased gating currents, see Fig. 2) suggests differences in the unitary current (e.g. increase in single-channel conductance or open probability or prolonged open times). Single-channel analysis will also be required to provide a mechanistic explanation for the shift in activation gating parameters in the short CaV1.3 channels. Changes in the unitary conductance of CaV1.2 channels induced by structural alterations within the CaM interaction domains in the C terminus well outside the known pore-forming regions have been described (38) providing evidence for a conformational link of the CaM binding domain not only with the gating machinery but also with the channel pore.
###end p 83
###begin p 84
###xml 228 229 228 229 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">V</sub>
###xml 232 234 232 234 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">42</sub>
###xml 317 319 317 319 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="ref39">39</xref>
###xml 356 358 356 358 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="ref40">40</xref>
###xml 385 387 385 387 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="ref41">41</xref>
###xml 446 447 446 447 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">V</sub>
###xml 653 655 653 655 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="ref42">42</xref>
###xml 670 672 670 672 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="ref43">43</xref>
###xml 815 816 815 816 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">V</sub>
###xml 974 975 974 975 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">V</sub>
###xml 986 988 986 988 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="ref35">35</xref>
Several proteins binding to interaction domains of the C terminus have been described. For example, in neurons the multifunctional PDZ protein Erbin binds to a PDZ-binding domain formed by the last four amino acid residues of CaV1.342 and thereby relieves the autoinhibitory effect of the C terminus on VDF (see Ref. 39 and see above). Also Shank protein (40) and RIM-binding protein (41) are predicted to bind selectively to the C terminus of CaV1.3 channels and may therefore affect CTM function. The fact that the distal C terminus comprising the CTM also contains regulatory sites, including sites for cAMP-dependent protein kinase phosphorylation (42) and AKAP-15 (43), raises the interesting possibility that their interference with the activity of the CTM itself could also serve as mechanism to fine-tune CaV1.3 function. Such a mechanism has recently been proposed to explain the still enigmatic mechanism of cAMP-dependent protein kinase-dependent activation of CaV1.2 LTCCs (35).
###end p 84
###begin p 85
###xml 60 61 60 61 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">V</sub>
###xml 117 119 117 119 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 247 248 247 248 <sub xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">V</sub>
###xml 327 329 327 329 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 360 361 360 361 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="ref7">7</xref>
###xml 392 393 392 393 <xref xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="ref8">8</xref>
###xml 767 769 767 769 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
Taken together, we have identified a modulatory domain in CaV1.3 LTCCs responsible for profound differences in the Ca2+- and voltage-dependent gating of the different C-terminal splice variants. Given that many unique physiological functions of CaV1.3 depend on the negative activation range of the channel and the amount of Ca2+ ions entering during plateau (7) or single action potentials (8), our findings identify the CTM and factors that modify its activity (such as alternative splicing) as elements suitable to determine electrical excitability. Future experiments must also address the interesting possibility that interference with this C-terminal regulatory mechanism can also be exploited for pharmacological intervention, as an alternative to classical Ca2+ channel modulators.
###end p 85
###begin p 86
###xml 51 56 <span type="species:ncbi:10090">mouse</span>
###xml 235 240 <span type="species:ncbi:10090">mouse</span>

###end p 86
###begin p 87
###xml 43 45 43 45 <sup xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>

###end p 87
